Question Text:
Here's a fun riddle that I think you'll enjoy.

You have been selected to play the final round of the hit new game show "Pick That Ping-Pong". In this round, you will be competing for a large cash prize. Your job will be to pick one of several different numbered ping-pong balls, and then the game will commence. The host describes how the game works.

A device consisting of a winding clear ramp and a series of pistons controls the outcome of the game. The ramp feeds balls onto a platform. The platform has room for three ping-pong balls at a time. The three balls on the platform are each aligned with one of three pistons. At each stage of the game, one of the three pistons will randomly fire, ejecting the ball it strikes. If the piston ejects the ball in the first position on the platform the balls in the second and third position on the platform each advance one space, and the next ball on the ramp advances to the third position. If the piston ejects the ball in the second position, the ball in the first position is released and rolls away, the ball in the third position advances two spaces to occupy the first position, and the next two balls on the ramp advance to occupy the second and third positions on the platform. If the piston ejects the ball in the third position, the ball in the first position is released and rolls away, the ball in the second position advances one space to occupy the first position, and the next two balls on the ramp advance to occupy the second and third positions on the platform.

The ramp begins with 100 numbered ping-pong balls, arranged in ascending order from 1 to 100. The host activates the machine and the first three balls, numbered 1, 2, and 3, advance to the platform. Before the random firing of the pistons begins, you are asked which of the 100 balls you would like to pick. If your pick is ejected by one of the pistons, you win the grand prize, $10,000.

Which ball should you choose to maximize your odds of winning the big prize? Please provide your answer as the number of the ball selected.


============================================================
STEP 1
============================================================
Duration: 39.13s
Tokens: input=2965, output=3662

--- Model Output ---
Thought: I'll estimate each ball's chance of being ejected by simulating the described random process many times. For each trial, I'll simulate the ramp and platform dynamics exactly as specified, record every ball ejected by a piston, and accumulate counts. After many trials I'll compute estimated probabilities for balls 1..100 and return the ball with the highest estimated probability. I'll run a large number of trials (100,000) for reliable estimates, print the top few probabilities for inspection, and then return just the chosen ball number as the final answer.

<code>
import random
from collections import defaultdict

def simulate_one():
    # ramp: numbers 1..100
    ramp_idx = 3  # next index to draw from ramp (0-based), as platform initially has 1,2,3
    platform = [1,2,3]  # positions 0,1,2 correspond to p1,p2,p3
    ejected = []  # list of ball numbers ejected by pistons this run

    # helper to draw next ball from ramp
    def draw_next():
        nonlocal ramp_idx
        if ramp_idx < 100:
            val = ramp_idx + 1  # ball numbers are 1-based
            ramp_idx += 1
            return val
        return None

    # fill platform initially if ramp had fewer than 3 (not in this problem but keep general)
    while len(platform) < 3:
        nxt = draw_next()
        if nxt is None:
            break
        platform.append(nxt)

    # Continue while there is at least one ball on platform
    while platform:
        # choose a piston among available positions uniformly
        choice = random.randrange(len(platform))  # 0..len-1
        if choice == 0:

STEP 4

Duration: 48.15s
Tokens: input=7744, output=5552

--- Model Output ---
Thought: Analytically the probability a ball is ejected depends only on the position it occupies when on the platform. Let r1, r2, r3 be the probabilities of eventual ejection when a ball is in positions 1,2,3 respectively. From the transition rules:
- r1 = 1/3 (next firing either ejects it with prob 1/3 or releases it with prob 2/3).
- r2 = (1/3)r1 + (1/3)*1 + (1/3)r1 = 1/3 + (2/3)r1 = 5/9.
- r3 = (1/3)r2 + (1/3)r1 + (1/3)*1 = 17/27.

So r3 > r2 > r1, meaning a ball's chance is higher the later it is in the queue. Thus later-numbered balls are strictly better picks, and the best choice among 1..100 is the last ball, 100.

            found.add(kw)

        if kw in txt:
            snippet = re.findall(r".{0,60}"+re.escape(kw)+r".{0,60}", txt)
            print(name, kw, "examples:", snippet[:3])
print("Keywords found across pages:", sorted(found))

# Based on the video title/description and these pages, the species present are:
# Emperor (penguin), Adélie (penguin), Giant petrel (petrel) -> three distinct bird species.

Thought: I will search the downloaded PDF bytes for likely scene-heading markers (INT., EXT., MAZE, CORRIDOR, CASTLE, etc.) to locate the first scene heading exactly as written in the BBC Writers' Room "Heaven Sent" script. I'll print any matching lines I find.

<code>
# Search the previously downloaded PDF bytes (variable 'page') for scene heading markers
data = page  # 'page' was fetched earlier from the BBC Writers' Room PDF

# Ensure we treat as bytes; if it's a str, encode to latin-1 to preserve bytes
if isinstance(data, str):
    b = data.encode('latin-1', errors='ignore')
else:
    b = data

markers = [b"\nINT.", b"\nEXT.", b"\nINT/EXT.", b"\nFADE IN", b"\nCUT TO", b"\nSCENE", b"MAZE", b"CORRIDOR", b"CASTLE", b"GALLIFREY"]
found = {}
for m in markers:
    idx = b.find(m)
    if idx != -1:
        # extract a region around match for context
        start = max(0, idx-80)
        end = idx + 200
        context = b[start:end].decode('latin-1', errors='replace')
        found[m.decode('latin-1')] = context

# Also look for all uppercase lines that look like scene headings: lines with many uppercase letters and maybe dashes
lines = b.split(b'\n')
uppercase_candidates = []
for line in lines:
    s = line.decode('latin-1', errors='ignore').strip()
    if len(s) >= 4 and sum(1 for ch in s if ch.isupper() or ch in " .-/") / max(1,len(s)) > 0.6:
        uppercase_candidates.append(s)

# Print results
print("Marker contexts found:")
for k, v in found.items():
    print(f"--- {k} ---")
    print(v)
    print()

print("Uppercase candidate lines (first 40):")
for i, s in enumerate(uppercase_candidates[:40]):
    print(f"{i+1}: {s}")
</code>

from itertools import product
formulas = {
 "¬(A ∧ B) ↔ (¬A ∨ ¬B)": lambda A,B: (not (A and B)) == ((not A) or (not B)),
 "¬(A ∨ B) ↔ (¬A ∧ ¬B)": lambda A,B: (not (A or B)) == ((not A) and (not B)),
 "(A → B) ↔ (¬B → ¬A)": lambda A,B: ((not A) or B) == ((not (not B)) or (not A)),
 "(A → B) ↔ (¬A ∨ B)": lambda A,B: ((not A) or B) == ((not A) or B),
 "(¬A → B) ↔ (A ∨ ¬B)": lambda A,B: ((not (not A)) or B) == (A or (not B)),
 "¬(A → B) ↔ (A ∧ ¬B)": lambda A,B: (not ((not A) or B)) == (A and (not B))
}
non_tautologies = []
for name, fn in formulas.items():
    vals = [fn(A,B) for A,B in product([False, True], [False, True])]
    taut = all(vals)
    print(name, "->", vals, "tautology?", taut)
    if not taut:
        non_tautologies.append(name)
print("NON_TAUTOLOGIES:", non_tautologies)
""")
print(result)
</code>

I'll test the truth table for the suspicious formula (¬A → B) ↔ (A ∨ ¬B). If it's not a tautology while the others are, then it's the one that doesn't fit. I'll use evaluate_math to compute the truth table and check whether the biconditional holds for all assignments of A and B.

<code>
result = evaluate_math(expression="""
from itertools import product
def impl(p,q): return (not p) or q
non_tauto = []
# Check the specific formula (¬A → B) ↔ (A ∨ ¬B)
vals = []
for A,B in product([False, True], [False, True]):
    lhs = impl(not A, B)   # ¬A → B
    rhs = (A or (not B))   # A ∨ ¬B
    vals.append((A,B,lhs,rhs,lhs==rhs))
for row in vals:
    print(row)
print("All equal (tautology)?", all(r[-1] for r in vals))
""")
print(result)
</code>

Question Text:
My family reunion is this week, and I was assigned the mashed potatoes to bring. The attendees include my married mother and father, my twin brother and his family, my aunt and her family, my grandma and her brother, her brother's daughter, and his daughter's family. All the adults but me have been married, and no one is divorced or remarried, but my grandpa and my grandma's sister-in-law passed away last year. All living spouses are attending. My brother has two children that are still kids, my aunt has one six-year-old, and my grandma's brother's daughter has three kids under 12. I figure each adult will eat about 1.5 potatoes of mashed potatoes and each kid will eat about 1/2 a potato of mashed potatoes, except my second cousins don't eat carbs. The average potato is about half a pound, and potatoes are sold in 5-pound bags. How many whole bags of potatoes do I need? Just give the number.

============================================================

<code>

mother = 1
father = 1

print("pounds_needed:", pounds_needed)
print("bags_needed:", bags_needed)

</code>

Question Text:
In the fictional language of Tizin, basic sentences are arranged with the Verb first, followed by the direct object, followed by the subject of the sentence. I want to express my love for apples to my Tizin friend. 

The word that indicates oneself is "Pa" is the nominative form, "Mato" is the accusative form, and "Sing" is the genitive form. 

The root verb that indicates an intense like for something is "Maktay". When it is used in the present, it is used in it's root form, when it is used in the preterit past, it is "Tay", and when it is used in the imperfect past, it is "Aktay". It is used differently than in English, and is better translated as "is pleasing to", meaning that the thing doing the liking is actually the object of the sentence rather than the subject.

The word for apples is borrowed from English in Tizin, and so it is "Apple" is the nominative form, "Zapple" is the accusative form, and "Izapple" is the genitive form. 

Please translate "I like apples" to Tizin.


============================================================
STEP 1
============================================================
Duration: 11.30s
Tokens: input=2759, output=1289

--- Model Output ---
Thought:
Tizin word order is Verb + direct object + subject. The verb "Maktay" (present) means "is pleasing to" so the English "I like apples" corresponds to "Apples are pleasing to me" — apples are the subject (nominative "Apple") and "me" is the direct object/experiencer (accusative "Mato"). Putting verb first, then accusative (Mato), then nominative (Apple) gives the Tizin sentence.

<code>
translation = "Maktay Mato Apple"
print(translation)
final_answer(translation)
</code>

<code>
import re

matches = []
# search for explicit "dinosaur" mentions
for m in re.finditer(r".{0,60}\bdinosaur\b.{0,60}", page_text, flags=re.IGNORECASE):
    matches.append(("dinosaur", m.group(0)))

# search for names ending in -saurus (common dinosaur suffix)
for m in re.finditer(r".{0,60}\b[A-Z][A-Za-z-]{3,}saurus\b.{0,60}", page_text):
    matches.append(("saurus", m.group(0)))

# search for common dinosaur article titles in the text (a few known ones)
for name in ["Tyrannosaurus", "Triceratops", "Velociraptor", "Giganotosaurus", "Stegoceras", "Allosaurus", "Apatosaurus"]:
    for m in re.finditer(r".{0,80}\b" + re.escape(name) + r"\b.{0,80}", page_text):
        matches.append((name, m.group(0)))

# print unique matches
seen = set()
for kind, ctx in matches:
    if ctx not in seen:
        seen.add(ctx)
        print(f"Match kind={kind} context:\n...{ctx}...\n\n") 
</code>

        dinosaur_candidates.append((article, date, nominator))
        print(f"Likely dinosaur article: {article} (nominator: {nominator})")

print("Dinosaur candidates found:", dinosaur_candidates)

Est. 1828

Word of the Day
===============

 : June 27, 2022

jingoism
--------

[play](javascript:void(0) "Listen to the pronunciation of jingoism")

noun

JING-goh-iz-im

[Prev](/word-of-the-day/culminate-2022-06-26)
[Next](/word-of-the-day/fulsome-2022-06-28)

---

What It Means
-------------

*Jingoism* is excessive patriotism or nationalism, especially when marked by a belligerent foreign policy.

// When the war began many people were caught up in a wave of *jingoism*.

[See the entry >](https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/jingoism)

jingoism in Context
-------------------

"War is bad for culture. Not least of all because it turns our cultural institutions into bastions of *jingoism*." — Annie Levin, *The New York Observer*, 7 Mar. 2022

|*|a|b|c|d|e|
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
|a|a|b|c|b|d|
|b|b|c|a|e|c|
|c|c|a|b|b|a|
|d|b|e|b|e|d|
|e|d|b|a|d|c|

provide the subset of S involved in any possible counter-examples that prove * is not commutative. Provide your answer as a comma separated list of the elements in the set in alphabetical order.

============================================================
Duration: 10.61s
Tokens: input=2668, output=1219

--- Model Output ---
Thought:
I'll load the Cayley table into Python, compare x*y with y*x for each unordered pair, collect elements involved in any mismatch, and return them in alphabetical order.

<code>
# Build the operation table as a dict of dicts: table[row][col] = result
table = {
    'a': {'a':'a','b':'b','c':'c','d':'b','e':'d'},
    'b': {'a':'b','b':'c','c':'a','d':'e','e':'c'},
    'c': {'a':'c','b':'a','c':'b','d':'b','e':'a'},
    'd': {'a':'b','b':'e','c':'b','d':'e','e':'d'},
    'e': {'a':'d','b':'b','c':'a','d':'d','e':'c'},
}

S = ['a','b','c','d','e']
mismatch_elements = set()

for i in range(len(S)):
    for j in range(i+1, len(S)):
        x = S[i]
        y = S[j]
        if table[x][y] != table[y][x]:
            mismatch_elements.add(x)
            mismatch_elements.add(y)

# Print mismatches for observation
print("Mismatching unordered pairs involve elements:", sorted(mismatch_elements))

# Prepare final answer in alphabetical order, comma separated
answer = ", ".join(sorted(mismatch_elements))
final_answer(answer)
</code>

The host has 30 shiny prop coins, each of which is worth $1,000 if Bob manages to win them by playing the game. The host hides the coins in three different prize boxes and then shuffles their order. The only rule restricting the host's coin placement is that one box must contain at least 2 coins, and one box must contain 6 more coins than another box. In order to play, Bob must submit three guesses, one guess for the number of coins in each box. The box is then opened and the number of coins is revealed. If Bob's guess is a number greater than the number of coins in the box, Bob earns no coins. If Bob guesses a number equal to or less than the number of coins in the box, Bob wins a number of coins equal to his guess.

                    ok = True
        if not ok:

        valid_S.append(S)

    total = 0

        if g <= c:
            total += g
        else:
            total += 0
    return total

3. Rule 601. Competency to Testify in General

Rule 601. Competency to Testify in General
==========================================

Every person is competent to be a witness unless these rules provide otherwise. But in a civil case, state law governs the witness’s competency regarding a claim or defense for which state law supplies the rule of decision.

#### Notes

(Pub. L. 93–595, §1, Jan. 2, 1975, 88 Stat. 1934; Apr. 26, 2011, eff. Dec. 1, 2011.)

Notes of Advisory Committee on Proposed Rules

This general ground-clearing eliminates all grounds of incompetency not specifically recognized in the succeeding rules of this Article. Included among the

import re

# Find the article headings and their rule lists from the printed text structure
# We'll extract the lines that look like "1. ARTICLE I. ..." and subsequent rule lines
text = soup.get_text("\n")
lines = [ln.strip() for ln in text.splitlines() if ln.strip()]

# Collect articles and their rule titles by parsing the pattern "ARTICLE <Roman>. <NAME>" and following numbered rules
articles = {}
current_article = None
for ln in lines:
    # Match article header like "1. ARTICLE I. GENERAL PROVISIONS"
    m = re.match(r'^\d+\.\s+ARTICLE\s+([IVXLC]+)\.\s+(.*)$', ln)
    if m:
        art_num = m.group(1).strip()
        art_name = m.group(2).strip()
        current_article = f"ARTICLE {art_num}. {art_name}"
        articles[current_article] = []
        continue
    # Match rule lines like "1. [Rule 101](/rules/fre/rule_101). Scope; Definitions"
    m2 = re.match(r'^\d+\.\s+\[Rule\s+(\d+)\].*\.\s*(.*)$', ln)
    if m2 and current_article:
        rule_num = m2.group(1)
        rule_title = m2.group(2).strip()
        articles[current_article].append((rule_num, rule_title))

# Count occurrences of the exact word "witnesses" in titles for each article (case-insensitive)
counts = {}
for art, rules in articles.items():
    cnt = 0
    for rn, title in rules:
        if re.search(r'\bwitnesses\b', title, flags=re.I):
            cnt += 1
    counts[art] = cnt

print("Counts of 'witnesses' in titles by article:")
for art, cnt in counts.items():
    print(f"{ar

        art_num = m.group(1).strip()
        art_name = m.group(2).strip()
        current_article = f"ARTICLE {art_num}. {art_name}"
        articles[current_article] = []
        continue
    # Match rule lines like "1. [Rule 101](/rules/fre/... [truncated]

List of Presidential Birthplaces — Roadtrip to their Birthplaces, Historic Sites, and Homes
===========================================================================================

* [Cards](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes?layout=nomap#cards)
* [List](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes?layout=nomap#sitelist)
* [Map](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes)

#### Let us look and take a tour across the country to see the birthplaces of the 46 United States Presidents. Look back into the yesteryears and visit the presidential birthplaces and early childhood historic sites.

#### Find out where the Presidents of the United States were born and their past before becoming a great part of our history.

#### If you have any suggestions or additional information related to this post, please add them in the comments below or [send us a](mailto:Editors@TheHistoryList.com) [message](http://Editors@TheHistoryList.com). If we publish a trip as a result, we'll include your name in the credits.

#### **GEORGE WASHINGTON** (February 22, 1732)

#### [George Washington Birthplace National Monument](https://www.thehistorylist.com/sites/george-washington-birthplace-national-monument-colonial-beach-virginia/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes-c8e604fc-ba01-4d1f-8a27-215bd7094917)

One of the Founding Fathers of the new United States of America and the nation’s first president, George Washington was born on February 22, 1732, at Pope’s Creek Estate, near to what is now Colonial Beach in Westmoreland County, Virginia. The site is now the [George Washington Birthplace National Monument](https://www.thehistorylist.com/sites/george-washington-birthplace-national-monument-colonial-beach-virginia/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes-c8e604fc-ba01-4d1f-8a27-215bd7094917), which the public can visit and learn more about our first president’s past. It is a beautiful 550 acres of the former Pope’s Creek Plantation, the ancestral dwelling of the Washington family. The place also offers various interesting sites and activities for the whole family, such as historic structures, picnic grounds, a visitor center, a colonial-inspired fa

Father Son and Holy Ghost
=========================

F

[![Audre Lorde](/storage/poets/resize/150x150_Mucm2fiEV5KhSB2HtZMj4ei3pQWw2UuK3eIpiYPr.jpeg) By

Audre Lorde](/poets/wgti-audre-lorde/ "Audre Lorde")

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

  

Not that his judgment eyes

have been forgotten

nor his great hands’ print

on our evening doorknobs

one half turn each night

and he would come

drabbled with the world’s business

massive and silent

as the whole day’s wish

ready to redefine

each of our shapes

but now the evening doorknobs

wait and do not recognize us

as we pass.

  

Each week a different woman

regular as his one quick glass

each evening

pulls up the grass his stillness grows

calling it weed.

Each week a different woman

has my mother’s face

and he

who [time](/category/time/ "time") has changeless

must be amazed

who knew and loved

but one.

  

My [father](/category/father/ "father") died in silence

loving creation

and well-defined response

he lived still judgments

on familiar things

and died knowing

a [january](/category/january/ "january") 15th that year me.

  

Lest I go into dust

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

3.1K

Rating:

Oh! yet one smile, tho' dark may lower  
 Around thee clouds of woe and ill,  
 Let me yet feel that I have power,  
 Mid Fate's bleak storms, to soothe thee still.  
  
 Tho' sadness be upon thy brow,  
 Yet let it turn, dear love, to me,  
 I cannot bear that thou should'st know  
 Sorrow I do not share with thee.

[### Audre Lorde](/audre-lorde)

Father Son and Holy Ghost
-------------------------

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

Not that his judgment eyes

have been forgotten

nor his great hands’ print

on our evening doorknobs

one half turn each night

and he would come

drabbled with the world’s business

massive and silent

as the whole day’s wish

ready to redefine

each of our shapes

but now the evening doorknobs

wait    and do not recognize us

as we pass.

Each week a different woman

regular as his one quick glass

each evening

pulls up the grass his stillness grows

calling it weed.

Each week    a different wo

There Are Hundreds of Mysterious Filaments at the Center of the Milky Way
=========================================================================

By [Carolyn Collins Petersen](/authors/cc-petersen.html) - June 06, 2023 06:00 PM UTC | [Milky Way](/categories/milky-way.html)

[![](/article_images/MeerKAT_and_bubbles_composite-1030x618-1.png)](/article_images/MeerKAT_and_bubbles_composite-1030x618-1.png)

Several million years ago, the core of our galaxy experienced a powerful event. It blew out a huge bi-lobed bubble that blasted through the interstellar medium in two directions. Whatever it was, it released huge amounts of energy from the central supermassive black hole, Sagittarius A\* (Sgr A\* for short).

These bubbles contain weird, spindly one-dimensional vertical filaments that emit radio signals of their own. In 1984, astronomer Farhad Yusef-Zadeh at Northwestern University and his collaborators were [the first to observe these wispy trails](https://www.universetoday.com/154593/imaging-the-galaxys-centre-in-unprecedented-detail-reveals-more-mysterious-filaments/). Now, they know of more than a thousand of them. They give off radio waves in the microwave portion of the electromagnetic spectrum.

[![MeerKAT image of the galactic center with color-coded position angles of the vertical filaments. Courtesy Farhad Yusef-Zadeh/Northwestern University](/article_images/2cd8b0ca-e457-417f-abd6-f7cecf5b03fd.png)](/article_images/2cd8b0ca-e457-417f-abd6-f7cecf5b03fd.png)

Wait, There's More!
-------------------

Recently, the team found a smaller population of filaments near Sgr A\*. However, these aren't vertical. Instead, they lie along the galactic plane and radiate away like spokes on a wheel. "It was a surprise to suddenly find a new population of structures that seem to be pointing in the direction of the black hole," Yusef-Zadeh said. "We have always been thinking about vertical filaments and their origin. I'm used to them being vertical. I never considered there might be others along the plane."

He and the team were stunned when they saw these radially stretched structures. "We had to do a lot of work to establish that we weren't fooling ourselves," he said. "And we found that these filaments are not random but appear to be tied to the outflow of our black hole. By studying them, we could learn more about the black hole's spin and accretion disk orientation. It is satisfying when one finds order in a middle of a chaotic field of the nucleus of our galaxy."

Yusef-Zadeh pointed out that something clearly occurred in the core to create the short
... [truncated]

<code>
import re
page = visit_webpage(url="https://clinicaltrials.gov/study/NCT03411733")
# Print some context lines that mention "Enrollment" to locate the field
lines = page.splitlines()
for i, line in enumerate(lines):
    if "Enrollment" in line or "Actual Enrollment" in line or "Estimated Enrollment" in line:
        start = max(0, i-2)
        end = min(len(lines), i+3)
        print("\n".join(lines[start:end]))
        print("-----")
# Try to extract a numeric value near "Actual Enrollment"
m = re.search(r'Actual Enrollment[^0-9\n\r]*([0-9,]+)', page, re.IGNORECASE)
if m:
    print("EXTRACTED:", m.group(1))
else:
    # fallback: find any 'Enrollment' followed by a number
    m2 = re.search(r'Enrollment[^0-9\n\r]*([0-9,]+)', page, re.IGNORECASE)
    print("FALLBACK:", m2.group(1) if m2 else "No match")
</code>

url_xml = "https://clinicaltrials.gov/study/NCT03411733?displayxml=true"

# Search for Enrollment tags or EnrollmentInfo in the XML
m = re.search(r'<Enrollment[^>]*>([^<]+)</Enrollment>', xml_page, re.IGNORECASE)
if m:
    print("Enrollment tag value:", m.group(1).strip())
else:
    # Try alternative tags that may indicate actual enrollment
    m2 = re.search(r'<EnrollmentInfo>.*?<EnrollmentCount>([0-9,]+)</EnrollmentCount>.*?</EnrollmentInfo>', xml_page, re.DOTALL|re.IGNORECASE)
    if m2:
        print("EnrollmentCount:", m2.group(1))
    else:
        # Fallback: show snippets containing 'enroll' for manual inspection
        for line in xml_page.splitlines():
            if 'enroll' in line.lower() or 'enrol' in line.lower():
                print(line.strip())

Thought: I'll query the ClinicalTrials.gov API for the full study record for NCT03411733 (JSON format), parse the JSON, and extract the actual enrollment count (EnrollmentInfo / EnrollmentCount or Enrollment type 'Actual'). I'll print relevant fields to find the precise number.

<code>
import json
url_api = "https://clinicaltrials.gov/api/query/full_studies?expr=NCT03411733&min_rnk=1&max_rnk=1&fmt=json"
resp = visit_webpage(url=url_api)
print("LENGTH:", len(resp))
# Try to parse JSON
try:
    data = json.loads(resp)
    # Drill into the structure to find enrollment info
    studies = data.get("FullStudiesResponse", {}).get("FullStudies", [])
    if not studies:
        print("No studies found in API response")
    else:
        study = studies[0].get("Study", {})
        protocol_section = study.get("ProtocolSection", {})
        enrollment_info = protocol_section.get("EnrollmentInfo", {})
        print("EnrollmentInfo raw:", enrollment_info)
        # EnrollmentCount may be present
        enrollment_count = enrollment_info.get("EnrollmentCount")
        enrollment_type = enrollment_info.get("EnrollmentCountType")
        print("EnrollmentCount:", enrollment_count)
        print("EnrollmentCountType:", enrollment_type)
        # Fallback: search whole JSON string for 'enrollment'
        if not enrollment_count:
            s = json.dumps(data)
            import re
            m = re.search(r'"EnrollmentCount"\s*:\s*"?(\\d+)"?', s)
            if m:
                print("Found by regex:", m.group(1))
except Exception as e:
    print("JSON parse error or other:", e)
    # fallback: print resp for inspection
    print(resp[:2000])
</code>

Thought: I'll query the ClinicalTrials.gov API study_fields endpoint for NCT03411733 requesting the enrollment fields (EnrollmentCount and EnrollmentCountType), parse the JSON response, and extract the actual enrollment number.

<code>
import json
import re
url_api = "https://clinicaltrials.gov/api/query/study_fields?expr=NCT03411733&fields=EnrollmentCount,EnrollmentCountType&min_rnk=1&max_rnk=1&fmt=json"
resp = visit_webpage(url=url_api)
print("LENGTH:", len(resp))
try:
    data = json.loads(resp)
    fields = data.get("StudyFieldsResponse", {}).get("StudyFields", [])
    if fields:
        ef = fields[0]
        enrollment_count = ef.get("EnrollmentCount")
        enrollment_type = ef.get("EnrollmentCountType")
        print("EnrollmentCount raw:", enrollment_count)
        print("EnrollmentCountType:", enrollment_type)
        # enrollment_count may be a list or string; normalize to digits
        if isinstance(enrollment_count, list):
            enrollment_count = enrollment_count[0] if enrollment_count else None
        if enrollment_count:
            m = re.search(r'(\d+)', str(enrollment_count))
            if m:
                print("EXTRACTED:", m.group(1))
            else:
                print("No digits found in enrollment_count")
        else:
            print("No enrollment_count present")
    else:
        print("No StudyFields in response")
except Exception as e:
    print("Error parsing JSON:", e)
    print(resp[:1000])
</code>

Question Text:
A standard Rubik’s cube has been broken into cubes making up its sides. The cubes are jumbled, and one is removed. There are 6 cubes with one colored face, 12 edge cubes with two colored faces, and 8 corner cubes with three colored faces. All blue cubes have been found. All cubes directly left, right, above, and below the orange center cube have been found, along with the center cube. The green corners have all been found, along with all green that borders yellow. For all orange cubes found, the opposite face’s cubes have been found. The removed cube has two colors on its faces. What are they? Answer using a comma separated list, with the colors ordered alphabetically.

Thought: I'll model a standard Rubik's cube faces and edges programmatically, apply the clues to mark which edge cubes are found, and see which edge remains (the missing two-colored cube). I'll then output the colors in alphabetical order as required.

<code>
# Define cube faces and adjacencies for a standard color scheme:
# U = white, D = yellow, F = green, B = blue, L = orange, R = red
faces = {
    "white": ["green", "red", "blue", "orange"],   # U adjacent to F,R,B,L
    "yellow": ["green", "orange", "blue", "red"],  # D adjacent to F,L,B,R
    "green": ["white", "red", "yellow", "orange"], # F adjacent to U,R,D,L
    "blue": ["white", "orange", "yellow", "red"],  # B adjacent to U,L,D,R
    "orange": ["white", "green", "yellow", "blue"],# L adjacent to U,F,D,B
    "red": ["white", "blue", "yellow", "green"]    # R adjacent to U,B,D,F
}

# Build list of unique edges (unordered pairs)
edges = set()
for f, neighs in faces.items():
    for n in neighs:
        pair = tuple(sorted([f, n]))
        edges.add(pair)
edges = sorted(list(edges))

print("All edges (pairs of adjacent faces):")
for e in edges:
    print(e)

# Apply clues to mark found edges
found = set()

# 1) All blue cubes have been found -> all edges containing 'blue'
for e in edges:
    if 'blue' in e:
        found.add(e)

# 2) The cubes directly left, right, above, and below the orange center cube have been found,
#    along with the center cube.
#    That corresponds to all edges adjacent to 'orange' center: orange paired with its four neighbors
for neigh in faces['orange']:
    pair = tuple(sorted(['orange', neigh]))
    found.add(pair)

# 3) The green corners have all been found, along with all green that borders yellow.
#    The green-yellow edge must be found.
pair_gy = tuple(sorted(['green', 'yellow']))
found.add(pair_gy)

# 4) For all orange cubes found, the opposite face's cubes have been found.
#    Interpret as: for each found orange-X edge, the corresponding red-X edge is also found.
#    (
... [truncated]
